    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="dd44170ca29946c8b9e9986ed625899c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'aac0b182-97ab-4d97-9668-72c537aa58ba'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d30505899" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries are promising sustainable energy storage systems, owing to their low cost and high energy density. However, the existing cathode chemistries, particularly layered sodium transition metal oxides, exhibit restricted electrochemical performance, which hinders their extensive applications. To overcome challenges, originating from kinetically limited structural transformations, various cation substitutions are reported in the literature. Especially the partial Na–K replacement is discussed recently, which can increase bulk Na-diffusivity and suppress undesirable phase transitions. Here, a critical study of potassium doping in different layered materials P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O with redox-active Co (and Mn), and P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with Co activity is presented. It is found that the presence of K significantly enhances the moisture sensitivity: P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O is quickly formed in the air from K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, while Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> remains stable under the same conditions. In electrochemical Na-cells, a partial K–Na cation exchange in P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O occurs without applying current. The electrochemical performance of P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is slightly increased in comparison to K-free P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>O.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> from the literature, staying, however, behind the performance of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2.</sub> K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> shows weaker rate capability than isostructural Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, reflecting a negative impact of K on Na-diffusivity. Therefore, effect of K-incorporation in layered structures on the battery performance strongly depends on the cathode composition.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406384-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The choice of materials for battery application should consider environmental, financial and healthcare aspects. In this sense, cobalt is conventionally considered as a non-perspective material for next-generation Li-batteries and post-Li batteries: it is expensive in comparison to other transition metals such as manganese, iron and nickel, and very harmful to health. In <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202406384-tbl-0001">1</a>, we summarized a comparative analysis of technical data of cobalt together with “greener” transition metals Ni, Mn, and Ti.</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="adfm202406384-tbl-0001">

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Since cobalt is typically a byproduct of nickel and copper mining,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0018_R_d30505731e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Sustain. Min.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0019_R_d30505731e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Int. J. Life Cycle Assess.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is meaningful to consider cobalt and nickel together. Cobalt possesses a more negative impact to human health and the environment compared to nickel,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0003_R_d30505731e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Resour. Conserv. Recycl.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0004_R_d30505731e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Clean. Prod.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0020_R_d30505731e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Geochem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but with the co-mining the environmental balance is impacted by both metals. Given the lower amount of cobalt compared to nickel in the ores,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0018_R_d30505731e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Sustain. Min.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> more nickel than cobalt is mined and, therefore, the limitation factor for cobalt mining is the amount of nickel needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0021_R_d30505731e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the analytical report,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0013_R_d30505731e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Commun. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the production of nickel is expected to grow by 28–31 times between the years 2020 and 2050. Therefore, the production of cobalt will increase inevitably as well. The comparison of prices for nickel and cobalt discloses a surprising fact, namely a significantly higher cost for nickel (see Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202406384-tbl-0001">1</a>). Currently, 65% of produced nickel is needed for stainless steel production, while only 15% is utilized for batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0011_R_d30505731e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another crucial factor to the overall use of cobalt and nickel is further conditioning of the raw materials. Refining cobalt and using it as cobalt sulphate poses a high health risk due to its carcinogenic properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0019_R_d30505731e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Int. J. Life Cycle Assess.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0022_R_d30505731e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - AIP Conf. Proc.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the same is true for nickel regarding the environmental impact<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0023_R_d30505731e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Int. J. Environ. Res. Public. Health">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the risk of cancer from the processed nickel sulfate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0024_R_d30505731e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Cancer Res.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of cobalt-free materials<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0025_R_d30505731e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Appl. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> by increasing the nickel amount in electrode materials cannot really improve the environmental impact. Moreover, much more nickel would be needed to compensate the demand in battery materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0026_R_d30505731e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> if the overall cobalt share of 57% use in batteries would be reduced. Note that cobalt is also needed for other applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0027_R_d30505731e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the processing of cobalt will not stop, even with supposed less amounts in batteries, high standards for mining and processing of the nickel-cobalt-ores and processed materials are needed, especially regarding a steady growing demand of nickel until 2050s.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0028_R_d30505731e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Min. Metall. Explor.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the growing demand for nickel, the growing demand for cobalt could also be met as a byproduct and/or through exploring unused cobalt sides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0029_R_d30505731e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Resour. Conserv. Recycl.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides this, regarding the lower abundance of cobalt compared to nickel, manganese or titanium,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0001_R_d30505731e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Geochem. Int.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the recycling rate of cobalt must be increased to meet future demands together with reduced environmental risks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0025_R_d30505731e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Appl. Phys.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The other metals titanium and manganese are overwhelmingly used in other applications than batteries. For manganese, the most important product is stainless steel,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0030_R_d30505731e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - NeuroToxicology">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while titanium is widely used in the aerospace industry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0006_R_d30505731e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Minerals">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mining these materials have the same risks to the environment like the mining of other products, resulting in higher concentrations of these elements in the soils and water at the mining sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0031_R_d30505731e1022" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Environ. Sci. Pollut. Res.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both materials have an aspect to health, mainly by inhalation of manganese during welding<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0032_R_d30505731e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with a high distribution in the human body,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0033_R_d30505731e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Curr. Environ. Health Rep.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and handling of TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0034_R_d30505731e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Radiol. Oncol.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exposure could be minimized by implementing safety tools and regulations like ventilation, PPE, monitoring and physical distance to the product.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0035_R_d30505731e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Hazard. Mater. Lett.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>A detailed consideration of the above-discussed aspects makes questionable the complete elimination of cobalt for battery application, especially when cobalt demonstrates benefits in electrochemical energy storage in comparison to nickel. As a well-known example, LiCoO<sub>2</sub>-containing cathode materials for Li-batteries can be mentioned. Moreover, layered Co-oxides with sodium show promising characteristics in sodium batteries, which have been known since 1980s.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0036_R_d30505731e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is meaningful to continue with the design and development of Co-containing materials for Na-batteries, at least in the frame of the fundamental research. The main drawback of layered Na<sub>x</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> as Na-cathodes is multiple phase transformations as O3&nbsp;→&nbsp;O“3&nbsp;→&nbsp;P3&nbsp;→&nbsp;P”3&nbsp;→&nbsp;P″3, mainly of order–disorder nature, during charging (desodiation), which hinder Na-extraction and degrade rate capability properties. A partial cation substitution can stabilize the crystal structure, eliminating phase transformations. Consequently, substitution with a second cation type such as Fe, Mn, or Ti was reported in the literature. Here, Fe and Mn are redox-active and participate in the electrochemical reaction during charge and discharge. Compositions Na<sub>x</sub>(Co,Mn)O<sub>2</sub> were suggested as promising cathodes with a large capacity originating from the redox activity of both Co and Mn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0037_R_d30505731e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. C">37</a>-<a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0040_R_d30505731e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In contrast, Ti is mostly inert in the working potential window and serves solely as a structure stabilizer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0041_R_d30505731e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0042_R_d30505731e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the redox activity of substituted transition metal cations may not always be beneficial for the long-term cycling stability of the material, probably due to structural defects continuously accumulated in the material. Thus, the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox pair causes a structural distortion due to the formation of the Jahn–Teller-active Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<i>d</i><sup>4</sup>) cations, leading to local structural misfits. A similar can be suggested for Fe-substitution. Moreover, it is well-documented that the majority of layered materials with the P2-type structure (prismatic oxygen surrounding of Na cations, Na-deficiency in the pristine state), independently on the elemental composition, undergo a phase transformation P2&nbsp;→&nbsp;O2 upon desodiation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0043_R_d30505731e1092" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> when the Na-content is less than ≈33%. On the one hand, this transformation is very detrimental for long-term cycling due to severe structure reconstructions. On the other hand, decreasing the upper cut-off voltage to leave the remaining Na-content higher by 33% significantly limits the specific capacity.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, an approach to stabilize the P2 crystal structure even at a very low Na content in layered oxides was suggested in the work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0044_R_d30505731e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Comm.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It implies the introduction of bigger K-cations in some quantity between the metal-oxygen layers. These K-cations also increase the interlayer distance in the layered structure, thus facilitating cationic diffusion (pillar effect of K). The material of interest, K<sub>0.4</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, was tested as a cathode in Na-cells with a Na-electrolyte. As it was expected, alkali cations exchange was observed during the first several charge/discharge cycles, but afterward, a Na<sub>x</sub>K<sub>0.08</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composition was stabilized, and only sodium ions were (de)intercalated. This approach enabled a stable cycling behavior at the current density of 1C for at least 500 cycles. Both nickel and manganese cations participate in the charge compensation mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0044_R_d30505731e1125" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Comm.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strategy of K-presence in layered Na-cathodes was further conferred on P2-materials with only Mn as transition metal cations<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0045_R_d30505731e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Comm.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and a mixture of Mn/Fe/Al cations;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0046_R_d30505731e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energies">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> all these materials demonstrated impressive electrochemical characteristics.</p>
         
         <p>In the present work, we studied the impact of K-substitution into Co-containing layered oxides of two different types. Since Na<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>1-x</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compounds are well-studied in the literature as cathodes in Na-batteries, we decided to synthesize their K-analogs and explore them in Na-batteries. Analogously to Na<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>1-x</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, we expect for K-materials a redox-active behavior mostly for Co cations at high cell potentials. Accordingly, we synthesized and investigated P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P3-K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O (in our electrochemical studies x was close to 0.5). It is expected that during cycling in Na-cells, K ions are mostly replaced by Na ions to the level of thermodynamically stable compositions. In parallel, a P2-material with a low K-content, K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O, was synthesized and tested in Na-cells similarly to K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O. As a reference, P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was subjected to the same electrochemical tests. Here we suppose a stabilizing effect of Ti on the crystal structure without its redox activity. Although K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, having very close structures, cannot be directly compared in the first charging due to the different initial oxidation states of cobalt, further cycling in the same potential range mostly neglects the difference in the composition. The focus was put on the comparative electrochemical behavior of materials in a broad temperature range at various current densities, and underlying reaction processes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406384-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406384-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis of Materials and Their Stability in Air</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0040">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202406384-sec-0040-title">2.1.1 K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O</h4>
               
               <p>The crystal structure of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> depends on both the synthesis temperature and the holding time in the air. Synthesis at 900–930&nbsp;°C leads to the formation of a rhombohedral <i>P</i>3 modification while increasing temperature to 950&nbsp;°C results in a mixture of rhombohedral <i>P</i>3 and hexagonal <i>P</i>2 forms. A quenching from the synthesis temperature is needed for obtaining a single-phase sample. For all investigations, we used the rhombohedral form (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0001">1a–d</a>).</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/04f96836-ca4a-4ecc-bf8c-d597ca2da71a/adfm202406384-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/04f96836-ca4a-4ecc-bf8c-d597ca2da71a/adfm202406384-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/327b485c-a137-4907-9060-6cdae85e51d7/adfm202406384-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/04f96836-ca4a-4ecc-bf8c-d597ca2da71a/adfm202406384-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) X-ray diffraction patterns of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O (red) together with calculated diffractograms (black) and a difference between them (blue line), Co-K<sub>α1</sub> radiation, λ&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.7889&nbsp;Å. The high and non-flat baseline in the case of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> arises from the residual Ar in the capillary. b) Crystal structure of anhydrous K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (left) and simplified crystal structure of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O. c) Thermal lattice expansion of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O, evaluated from neutron powder diffraction data. d) Detailed structural information for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O from neutron powder diffraction data.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The structure of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> represents the ABC…ABC arrangement of oxygen layers, in which Co and Mn cations share octahedral positions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0001">1b,d</a>). Many other layered oxides with Co and Mn, which have K or Na between MO<sub>6</sub> slabs, are arranged on a similar matter. For example, K<sub>0.5</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has the same layered ABC…ABC setting and the <i>R</i>3<i>m</i> space group, which was studied as a cathode for K-batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0047_R_d30505731e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A very similar structure is adopted by Na-deficient Na<sub>0.65</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0041_R_d30505731e1448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the larger ionic radius of K in comparison to Na (1.38&nbsp;Å vs 1.02&nbsp;Å for octahedral environment),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0048_R_d30505731e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Acta Cryst. A">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the maximal K-amount in KO<sub>6</sub>-prisms per unit cell can hardly be higher than 0.5. The structural model was refined based on the powder neutron diffraction allowing precise determination of oxygen positions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The average (Co,Mn)─O interatomic distance of 1.9237(6)&nbsp;Å at 300&nbsp;K points to Mn<sup>3+</sup> (d<sup>4</sup>) and Co<sup>3+</sup> (d<sup>6</sup>) cations in a low-spin state. The (Co,Mn)─O bond lengths vary from 1.9192 to 1.9264&nbsp;Å between 15 and 475&nbsp;K, confirming the structural integrity in this temperature range.</p>
               
               <p>Storing the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material in air for several hours results in pronounced changes in the diffraction pattern. Thus, there is a shift of reflections to smaller 2Theta values in the XRD (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0001">1a</a>), pointing to increasing the unit cell metrics due to the intercalation of water molecules. Analysis of the NPD data confirmed the <i>R</i>3<i>m</i> space group for the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O material as well as a water amount close to 0.3 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). However, some additional reflections, especially between 15° and 40° 2θ, were observed in the NPD patterns, which match well with potassium hydrogencarbonate KHCO<sub>3</sub> (Kalicinite), ICSD 2074.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0049_R_d30505731e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Acta Cryst. B">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <p>Therefore, not a simple H<sub>2</sub>O intercalation into the structure occurs, but rather an H<sub>2</sub>O insertion and K<sup>+</sup> cations elimination from the structure could take place. K<sup>+</sup> cations react with moisture and CO<sub>2</sub> from the air and form KHCO<sub>3</sub>. This issue is well-known also for some layered Na-oxides<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0050_R_d30505731e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and is extremely time-dependent. Note that the much bigger sample for neutron diffraction measurements was stored overnight (15&nbsp;h) in the air in contrast to 5&nbsp;h stay for conventional samples.</p>
               
               <p>The main structural motive of the hydrate phase is very close to the water-free phase. In the refined structural model, we supposed that H<sub>2</sub>O (or H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>) and the remaining K occupy the same crystallographic site, leading to increasing the distance between layers due to the much bigger radius of the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule. It was not possible to determine the occupancy numbers for H<sub>2</sub>O and K, thermal displacement and the z-coordinate independently because of a high correlation between these parameters. Therefore, we fixed the K-occupancy to 0.5 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0001">1b</a>, second table), although it should be smaller due to the KHCO<sub>3</sub> formation. The average (Co,Mn)-O interatomic distance 1.9064(13)&nbsp;Å at 300&nbsp;K is lower than for anhydrous K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> of 1.9237(6)&nbsp;Å, which might point to some oxidation of Co and Mn cations. Both K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0</sub><sub>.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.3H<sub>2</sub>O show a similar thermal expansion behavior between 15 and 475&nbsp;K (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0001">1c</a>).</p>
               
               <p>Thermogravimetric analysis of the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample after air contact demonstrates a mass loss within two temperature ranges. The first process starts immediately after onset heating (80&nbsp;°C) and finishes at ≈210&nbsp;°C. It is accompanied by an endothermic signal in the DTA curve with its maximum at 180&nbsp;°C. The second mass loss occurs between 750° and 800&nbsp;°C, also with an endothermic signal in the DTA curve. The low-temperature effect corresponds to water evaporation while the effect at high temperatures could correspond to evaporation of K and O as well as thermal decomposition of formed KHCO<sub>3</sub>. The overall mass loss below 220&nbsp;°C, re-calculated as a water content, corresponds to the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.4H<sub>2</sub>O composition, close to the estimated composition value from NPD. The process of H<sub>2</sub>O-insertion into K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is reversible: re-heating of the material at 250&nbsp;°C (in the air) after room-temperature air contact leads again to the formation of a water-free K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, as confirmed by XRD analysis.</p>
               
               <p>We measured a weight increase of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in air, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0002">2b</a>. A very fast mass rise is observed during the first two hours. After 5&nbsp;h, the sample weight is stabilized without further increase, corresponding to the overall composition “K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O”, without considering the KHCO<sub>3</sub> formation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0002">2b</a>). Water intercalation into K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was also studied in situ using XRD analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0002">2c</a>). Considering the evolution of 003 and 006 Bragg reflections, one can conclude a step-wise insertion process <i>vi</i>a an intermediate phase. In some experiments, we observed even co-existence of three different phases since all three 003 Bragg reflections were present.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a4a44929-1649-4fec-bcf6-9583c952dae1/adfm202406384-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/a4a44929-1649-4fec-bcf6-9583c952dae1/adfm202406384-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ccda9629-319d-4d2a-bf2d-2423c16b4902/adfm202406384-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a4a44929-1649-4fec-bcf6-9583c952dae1/adfm202406384-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TG-DTA curves of water-containing K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O material, recorded in dry air upon increasing temperature with 10&nbsp;K&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>. b) Weight increase of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> due to water absorption during storage in air, calculated as a water content per K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> formula unit. c) In situ XRD studies of water intercalation into K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2,</sub> shown as a counter-plot for 003 and 006 Bragg reflections (Co-K<sub>α1</sub> radiation).</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The chemical composition of the materials was confirmed by the ICP-OES analysis: it corresponds to K<sub>0.46(2)</sub>Co<sub>0.31(2)</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for the dried material, and the same K:Co:Mn atomic ratio was obtained for the water-containing sample.</p>
               
               <p>From the results of the synthesis and materials characterization, we can conclude a high reactivity of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> toward replacement/intercalation reactions due to the layered character of the structure and apparently not very high thermodynamic stability. Therefore, additional measures should be undertaken to protect the material during storage. Furthermore, low structural stability can be expected upon electrochemical treatments.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0050">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202406384-sec-0050-title">2.1.2 Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h4>
               
               <p>As an alternative to rhombohedral structures with the redox-active Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> pairs and K cations between metal-oxygen layers, which should be replaced by Na cations during operation in Na-cells, we also investigated K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a material with a small degree of K-substitution. The structural information for K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0003">3</a>; the XRD pattern of K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Both materials crystallize in the same <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> space group, similar to P2-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> from the previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0051_R_d30505731e1859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energies">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We suppose that K-cations are equally distributed on the Na-positions. Introducing K-cations into the crystal structure results in increasing the lattice parameter “<i>c</i>” and in a minor decrease of the “<i>a</i>” parameter. Both materials do not react with moisture in the air at least for 15&nbsp;h, as confirmed by in situ XRD measurements (data not shown). Apparently, the introduction of a small amount of K cations into the structure leads to a slight structural distortion: refined values of thermal displacement parameters are higher in the case of the K-containing material. Both Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are stable in air at least up to 550&nbsp;°C. Above this temperature, a slight mass loss begins, possibly due to sodium and/or oxygen elimination. Therefore, these materials exhibit a lower reactivity (and a higher thermodynamic stability) in comparison to K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/229228b5-9ca0-44ae-8e10-3d925c9f1f65/adfm202406384-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/229228b5-9ca0-44ae-8e10-3d925c9f1f65/adfm202406384-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f949e67f-ab01-47f3-a8c1-006d1b149287/adfm202406384-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/229228b5-9ca0-44ae-8e10-3d925c9f1f65/adfm202406384-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Crystal structure of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. b) XRD pattern of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (red) together with the calculated diffractogram (black) and a difference between them (blue line), Co-K<sub>α1</sub> radiation, λ&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.7889&nbsp;Å. c) TG-DTA curve of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, recorded in a dried air. d) Structural data for Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406384-sec-0060-title">2.2 Electrochemical Studies</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0070">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202406384-sec-0070-title">2.2.1 Chemical Stability in a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> Electrolyte</h4>
               
               <p>Prior to electrochemical measurements, the chemical stability of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte solution was evaluated after storage in the electrolyte for 40&nbsp;h, using XRD measurements (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0004">4</a>). During XRD, the samples were not completely isolated from the air. Obviously, both materials show the same instability in the air like pristine K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, as seen from the changes of the 003 reflection positions with time.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a8a67626-9810-42c9-873c-e608e05ceef3/adfm202406384-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/a8a67626-9810-42c9-873c-e608e05ceef3/adfm202406384-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ec6e0762-5546-49ce-b52b-4ab8eecbdd81/adfm202406384-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a8a67626-9810-42c9-873c-e608e05ceef3/adfm202406384-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Part of XRD patterns of a) K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and b) K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O after storage in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution in EC&nbsp;+&nbsp;PC, presented as counterplots. The materials were measured in Kapton foils, which do not completely protect the samples from contact with air.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Nevertheless, from the XRD data, we can conclude the following. 1) Already in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte solution, the K→ Na cation exchange occurs, since we see very pronounced reflections of orthorhombic KClO<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0052_R_d30505731e2068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Acta Cryst. B">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is less soluble in carbonate-based electrolytes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0053_R_d30505731e2073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Phys. Chem. B">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in comparison to NaClO<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0054_R_d30505731e2081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Electrochim. Acta">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) At the beginning of the XRD measurement, the 003-reflections of both K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O correspond to lower <i>d</i>-values in comparison to the pristine state, pointing to the presence of smaller Na-cations between layers. Thus, 003 is related to <i>d</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.38&nbsp;Å in the initial K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and to <i>d</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5.90&nbsp;Å in K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> after electrolyte storage. For K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O, the 003-reflection corresponded to <i>d</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.95&nbsp;Å in the pristine state and decreased to <i>d</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.4&nbsp;Å after electrolyte storage. Apparently, Na-substitution takes place spontaneously without electrochemical treatments. During the XRD measurements, the new 003* and 006* reflections appeared and/or grew in both samples due to a spontaneous reaction with water molecules. Note that 003* and 006* were already present in the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O sample when XRD was started, which probably implies a higher reactivity of (Na,K)<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O toward H<sub>2</sub>O molecules. Therefore, a Na-containing (Na,K)<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase seems to be thermodynamically more stable than K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (spontaneous cation exchange in the electrolyte solution), but still less stable than water-containing K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*xH<sub>2</sub>O, because at the end of the XRD measurement, a phase with <i>d</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.95&nbsp;Å is formed in both samples, which has very probably the (Na,K)<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*yH<sub>2</sub>O composition.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0080">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202406384-sec-0080-title">2.2.2 Galvanostatic Cycling and Rate Capability Studies</h4>
               
               <p>The impact of the crystal structure and chemical composition on the electrochemical performance was tested using galvanostatic cycling at a 0.1C current density and rate capability tests at various current densities and different temperatures. Four materials K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2,</sub> K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O, K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were galvanostatically polarized in the potential window between 4.2 and 1.6&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na; the second charge–discharge cycle and the discharge capacity at 0.1C for the first 25 cycles are shown as examples in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0005">5</a>. For all materials, capacity development with the cycle number shows generally the same tendency (a weak capacity loss). It is difficult to distinguish here between the contribution from the cathode material and the degradation of metallic Na-anode. Interestingly, all K-containing materials demonstrate at the beginning of cycling a short capacity rise, followed by a decreasing capacity. This effect was observed for other layered K-oxides in Na-batteries,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0044_R_d30505731e2265" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Comm.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and could be explained by decreasing the real mass of the active material due to the replacement of the heavier K cations by lighter Na cations.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e713b49-0d61-46f3-874b-eda6a1e05614/adfm202406384-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e713b49-0d61-46f3-874b-eda6a1e05614/adfm202406384-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c89606fe-7841-4207-a13b-63aa60a0d639/adfm202406384-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e713b49-0d61-46f3-874b-eda6a1e05614/adfm202406384-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charge-discharge curves of the second cycle, and b) discharge capacity values versus cycle number at 0.1C current rate for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2,</sub> K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O, K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>In rate capability studies (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0006">6</a>), the electrochemical performance of all materials at 40&nbsp;°C is very close to or even lower than the performance at 25&nbsp;°C, emphasizing a negative impact of other battery cell components, such as electrolyte solution or metallic sodium, for the specific capacity. At room temperature, the ratio of the capacity at 1C to the one at 0.1C corresponds to 70% for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 67% for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O, 71% for K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and shows a much higher value of 87% for Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. This difference refers to bulk diffusion properties of the cathode material since other parameters were kept the same in the battery cells. Furthermore, at a high current density of 1C, the difference in the capacity values between 25 and 0 or 5&nbsp;°C is the smallest for Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (25&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), in comparison to 30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 40&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O and 30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. At low temperatures (0–10&nbsp;°C), K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O showed the strongest capacity degradation among other materials, even at the 0.1C current rate. Therefore, the presence of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules between metal-oxygen layers has the most detrimental influence on diffusion phenomena at low temperatures. The presence of a small quantity of bigger K-cations in the K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structure does not improve cation diffusion in the layered structure, neither in terms of the capacity value nor in terms of the rate capability. Nevertheless, the healing effect after tests at different current densities is outstanding for all four materials.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3518a050-9a9c-4ca9-a937-42e7dea2533e/adfm202406384-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/3518a050-9a9c-4ca9-a937-42e7dea2533e/adfm202406384-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a4942aa4-582d-46f6-b44d-108d986e20dc/adfm202406384-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3518a050-9a9c-4ca9-a937-42e7dea2533e/adfm202406384-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rate capability properties of layered Co-containing oxides K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and K<sub>0.1</sub>Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at various temperatures. Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> demonstrates the best performance at all temperatures. The healing effect is outstanding for all materials during operation below 40&nbsp;°C.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0090">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202406384-sec-0090-title">2.2.3 Impact of the Electrolyte Composition</h4>
               
               <p>As discussed above, rate capability experiments showed deterioration of the electrochemical performance for all studied materials at 40&nbsp;°C, which can be related among others to insufficient properties of the electrolyte. Therefore, we focused on two conventional electrolyte formulations, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC + PC + 5%FEC and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC + DEC, and comprehensively tested them with the Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode, which provides the best performance.</p>
               
               <p>A very low current density of 0.02C is close to equilibrium conditions and enables to reach capacities, close to the theoretical values. Simultaneously, it should promote secondary side reactions, especially at elevated temperatures. Indeed, the shape of charge–discharge curves of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is very similar in both electrolyte formulations at 25&nbsp;°C, and noticeably differs at 40&nbsp;°C, see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <p>As seen from <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0007">7a</a>, at 25&nbsp;°C the capacity of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can achieve 120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the 4.2–1.6&nbsp;V potential window versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, what corresponds to the change in the Na-composition Δx(Na)&nbsp;≈&nbsp;0.5 per formula unit in the second and following cycles. The Coulombic efficiency is almost 100% (data not shown), and the cycling is quite stable. At 40&nbsp;°C, the discharge capacity exceeds 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycles, which corresponds to the Coulombic efficiency of higher than 130%. This value decreases to nearly 100% to the 32th cycle, and the capacity reaches ≈120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, similar to the behavior at 25&nbsp;°C. Afterward, the cycling becomes very unstable, pointing to a possible electrical contact problem in the battery cell. The enhanced discharge capacity at 40&nbsp;°C could originate from both, a redox instability of the electrolyte (parasitic reactions of electrolyte components with metallic Na) and/or an undesirable excessive Na-insertion into Na<sub>x≈0.7-0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> below 2.35&nbsp;V, visible as a plateau-like step followed by a sloping region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). From the previous work<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0051_R_d30505731e2595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energies">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is known that Na<sub>0.8</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can intercalate ≈0.2Na per formula unit at low potentials; this insertion represents a two-phase reaction process, and a new isostructural P2*-Na<sub>x≈1.0</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase with a bigger unit cell volume is formed. In this case, a very slow kinetics of the cobalt reduction from Co<sup>3+</sup> to Co<sup>2+</sup>, which is accompanied by a structural transformation P2&nbsp;→&nbsp;P2*, is responsible for a fast capacity loss, observed in.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0051_R_d30505731e2622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energies">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At room temperature and a 0.02C current density, this transition occurs below 1.6&nbsp;V. At 40&nbsp;°C, this transition could be initiated at a higher cell potential. However, if we use 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> as an electrolyte, no additional plateau-like regions are visible down to 1.6&nbsp;V versus Na at 40&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). In this electrolyte, the capacity values at 40&nbsp;°C are much lower than at room temperature, which should be clarified in the future.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1304dfcf-2ccb-44c5-aa62-caf4cce3adba/adfm202406384-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/1304dfcf-2ccb-44c5-aa62-caf4cce3adba/adfm202406384-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c535fe91-00c4-48c7-9862-a154a54863b0/adfm202406384-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1304dfcf-2ccb-44c5-aa62-caf4cce3adba/adfm202406384-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Galvanostatic cycling of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in NaClO<sub>4</sub> - and NaPF<sub>6</sub> -containing electrolytes at 25 and 40&nbsp;°C under a) 0.02C and b) 1C current densities, together with the Coulombic efficiency (CE). In the case of the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, increasing the temperature to 40&nbsp;°C enlarges the discharge capacity noticeably, while the capacity diminishes in the case of the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Therefore, the observed unstable cycling behavior of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> at 40&nbsp;°C primarly originates from the strongly temperature-dependent secondary reactions in the electrolyte solution and phase transformation in the cathode.</p>
               
               <p>Comparison of capacity values at the 1C current density for the first 100 cycles reveals a very stable behavior of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte with a Coulombic efficiency very close to 100% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0007">7b</a>). In contrast, a noticeable continuous capacity loss occurs in the cell with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>, which is accompanied by decreasing the Coulombic efficiency below 100%. Therefore, the charge capacity prevails more and more over the discharge one, pointing to some parasitic reactions at high cell voltages. The same effect (CE below 100%) was noticed also for the 0.02C current density.</p>
               
               <p>We performed post-mortem XPS measurements to understand the different behavior of the Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode in these electrolyte solutions, see <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0008">8</a>. Both cells were charged and discharged for 25 cycles at 0.1C. Afterward, the cathode materials were washed with DMC and transferred to the spectrometer without any contact with air. After cycling in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, a well-detectable Co2p spectrum of the cathode was observed, while for the cell with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, almost no Co could be detected on the electrode surface, pointing to a much thicker cathodic solid–electrolyte interface (SEI) for the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Further, in case of the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, more ionically conductive NaF is built on the surface, while more covalent C─F species are formed on the cathode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte as well as P─O components, probably from a partial NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0055_R_d30505731e2741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Oxygen species correspond to ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion (532.4&nbsp;eV) in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, probably as a salt rest. This is indirectly confirmed by a higher intensity of the NaKLL signal. Therefore, different cycling stability in NaClO<sub>4</sub> and NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes originates from the different compositions and thickness of the cathodic SEI: in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the thickness of the SEI is much lower and the SEI contains NaF, while a much thicker SEI in the case of NaPF<sub>6</sub> contains C–F-species.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e7b40b2-a36d-4a17-8d1b-80842fd7a20b/adfm202406384-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e7b40b2-a36d-4a17-8d1b-80842fd7a20b/adfm202406384-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a50d25dc-c8f6-412b-8788-a7ced9a8bebd/adfm202406384-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e7b40b2-a36d-4a17-8d1b-80842fd7a20b/adfm202406384-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS results of Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes after 25 cycles in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The formation of a thick surface layer, which impedes the transport of Na-cations, should be also reflected in a higher inner resistance of the battery, which was determined by EIS studies. We applied EIS measurements to two cells with the Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes, after the OCV regime, 5th and 55th charge–discharge cycles with the 1C current density (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Already after 3&nbsp;h OCV, a solid-electrolyte interface is formed in both cells (presence of the R<sub>SEI</sub> element). Here it should be noted that from the EIS measurements, it is difficult to extract information separately from the cathode and anode side. The electrolyte resistance was nearly constant for both cells, having the Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> working electrode with 1&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup> surface area, during the cycling. It was slightly lower for 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> than for 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> (10-11 vs 18–22 Ω). We focused on the charge transfer resistance values for both cells: for 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> it changed from 493 to 618 Ω between 5th and 55th cycles while for 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> it was lower, varying between 309 and 527 Ω. Therefore, a thicker cathodic SEI on Na<sub>0.7</sub>Co<sub>0.8</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> cell causes higher charge-transfer resistance, thus downgrading the cycling behavior at a high current density of 1C.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406384-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406384-sec-0100-title">2.3 Reaction Processes in the Cathode During Battery Operation</h3>
            
            <p>The K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O materials show different XRD patterns after 25 cycles in the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406384-fig-0009">9a,b</a>). For both samples, the first 003 reflection is shifted to bigger 2Theta values, pointing to a significant decreasing the distance between layers. Furthermore, both materials comprise a very similar group of reflections between 20 and 40° 2Theta. Their analysis disclosed the presence of KClO<sub>4</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0052_R_d30505731e2904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Acta Cryst. B">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in a noticeable amount in the samples. We already observed KClO<sub>4</sub> in the K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O materials after storage in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte solution. Obviously, the KClO<sub>4</sub> precipitate remains on the cathode surface during entire battery cycling. These results confirm the thermodynamically driven cation exchange K→Na in the layered structure, proposed in the work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406384-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406384-bib-0044_R_d30505731e2938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Comm.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As reported here, the K<sub>0.4</sub>[Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode loses about half of the K-amount already during the open circuit voltage (OCV) procedure in a Na-cell.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406384-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c84b7b8f-5feb-44db-8278-932ea3662d5e/adfm202406384-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c84b7b8f-5feb-44db-8278-932ea3662d5e/adfm202406384-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ae813ab-7d6b-437a-89eb-109421d4620c/adfm202406384-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c84b7b8f-5feb-44db-8278-932ea3662d5e/adfm202406384-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406384-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406384" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) X-ray diffraction patterns of K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>*0.5H<sub>2</sub>O materials before and after operation in Na-cells with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte solution.
